Question 0:
You are a linguist specializing in Yauyos Quechua. You are given a sentence along with its morpheme breakdown, gloss, and translation. Words are separated by spaces, and morphemes are separated by hyphens. However, a word and its gloss are missing and represented by an underscore. Based on your understanding, please choose the most appropriate option. 
Sentence (with missing item): ___ qati-pa-ru-n urqu-ta
Gloss (with missing item): ___ follow-REPET-URGT-3 hill-ACC
The English translation of this sentence is:His wife herded him back to the hills.
Here is a relevant knowledge point for this example, with the related morphemes and glosses masked: The class of regular nouns\index[sub]{nouns!regular} includes all nouns not included in the other three classes. Although in this sense it is defined negatively, as a kind of default class, it includes by far more members than any of the others. ~(\ref{Glo3:Warmi}--\ref{Glo3:Ukucha}) give examples.\\
A: word: warmi-n-pa	 gloss: woman-3-GEN
B: word: trupa-lla-n-ta	 gloss: tail-RSTR-3-ACC
C: word: warmi-n	 gloss: woman-3
D: word: warmi-n-pis	 gloss: woman-3-ACC
Please only return the letter (A–D). Do not say anything else.
Correct Answer: D

Here is a relevant knowledge point for this example, with the related morphemes and glosses masked: The class of regular nouns\index[sub]{nouns!regular} includes all nouns not included in the other three classes. Although in this sense it is defined negatively, as a kind of default class, it includes by far more members than any of the others. ~(\ref{Glo3:Warmi}--\ref{Glo3:Ukucha}) give examples.\\

Here is a relevant knowledge point for this example, with the related morphemes and glosses masked: Nouns referring to time\index[sub]{nouns!time} (\phono{kanan} ‘now’, \phono{wata} ‘year’) form a unique class in that they may occur adverbally without inflection, as in~(\ref{Glo3:Tukuy}--\ref{Glo3:Qaynahuk}).\\

Question 7:
You are a linguist specializing in Yauyos Quechua. You are given a sentence along with its morpheme breakdown, gloss, and translation. Words are separated by spaces, and morphemes are separated by hyphens. However, a word and its gloss are missing and represented by an underscore. Based on your understanding, please choose the most appropriate option. 
Sentence (with missing item): kay-lla-ta nisita-nki aw ___ llama wira-ta
Gloss (with missing item): DEM.P-RSTR-ACC need-2 yes ___ llama fat-ACC
The English translation of this sentence is:You need only this, uncle, llama fat?}%eng
Here is a relevant knowledge point for this example, with the related morphemes and glosses masked: Nouns indigenous to \SYQ{} do not inflect for gender\index[sub]{nouns!gender}. \SYQ{} indicates biological gender either with distinct noun roots (\phono{maqta} ‘young man’, \phono{pashña} ‘young woman’) or by modification with \phono{qari} ‘man’ or \phono{warmi} ‘woman’ in the case of people (\phono{qari wawa} ‘boy child’, \phono{warmi wawa} ‘girl child’) or \phono{urqu} ‘male’ or \phono{trina} ‘female’ in the case of animals. A few nouns, all borrowed from Spanish, are inflected for gender (masculine \pb{\textipa{/u/}} and feminine \textipa{/a/}).~(\ref{Glo3:Kayllata}--\ref{Glo3:Unayunay}) give examples.\\
A: word: qipi-ku-sa-kama	 gloss: carry-REFL-PRF-ALL
B: word: abuelo	 gloss: grandfather
C: word: tiyu-yki-pa	 gloss: uncle-2-LOC
D: word: tiyu	 gloss: uncle
Please only return the letter (A–D). Do not say anything else.
Correct Answer: D

Question 9:
You are a linguist specializing in Yauyos Quechua. You are given a sentence along with its morpheme breakdown, gloss, and translation. Words are separated by spaces, and morphemes are separated by hyphens. However, a word and its gloss are missing and represented by an underscore. Based on your understanding, please choose the most appropriate option. 
Sentence (with missing item): hinashpa-qa hatari-ru-: allqu-kuna ___ ka-ra
Gloss (with missing item): then-TOP get.up-URGT-1 dog-PL ___ be-PST
The English translation of this sentence is:Then I got up. Dogs were at his side.
Here is a relevant knowledge point for this example, with the related morphemes and glosses masked: Locative nouns\index[sub]{nouns!locative} indicate relative position (\phono{chimpa} ‘front’, \phono{hawa} ‘top’). They are inflected with the suffixes of the substantive (possessive) paradigm which indicate the person --~and, in the case of the first person, also the number~-- of the complement noun.~(\ref{Glo3:Hinas}--\ref{Glo3:Uma}) give examples.\\
A: word: law-pa	 gloss: side-LOC
B: word: hatari-na-n-kaman	 gloss: get.up-NMLZ-3-ALL
C: word: yata-n-pa	 gloss: side-3-LOC
D: word: yata-n-pi	 gloss: side-3-LOC
Please only return the letter (A–D). Do not say anything else.
Correct Answer: C

A: word: ñuqa-man-pis	 gloss: I-ALL-ADD
B: word: ñuqa-pa	 gloss: I-GEN
C: word: ñuqa-pis	 gloss: I-ADD
D: word: tilivisyun-ta	 gloss: television-ACC
Please only return the letter (A–D). Do not say anything else.

Question 19:

Question 28:
You are a linguist specializing in Yauyos Quechua. You are given a sentence along with its morpheme breakdown, gloss, and translation. Words are separated by spaces, and morphemes are separated by hyphens. However, a word and its gloss are missing and represented by an underscore. The English translation of this sentence is:In order to me able to eat me when he got back, he tied me up.
Here is a relevant knowledge point for this example, with the related morphemes and glosses masked: In the verbal and nominal paradigm tables, for reasons of space, I generally do not list \phono{ñuqa-nchik-kuna} and \phono{ñuqa-kuna} with the other first person pronouns in the headings; it can be assumed that the first patterns with \phononb{ñuqa}\phononb{-nchik}, the second with \phono{ñuqa}. In practice, where context does not adequately specify the referent, speakers of \SYQ{} make distinctions between the dual, inclusive and exclusive first-person plural exactly like speakers of English and Spanish do, indicating the dual, for example, with \phono{ishkay-ni-nchik} ‘the two of us’; the inclusive with \phono{llapa-nchik} ‘all of us’; and the exclusive with modifying phrases, as in \phono{ñuqa-nchik Viñac-pa} ‘we in Viñac’. \SYQ{} makes no distinction between subject, object~(\ref{Glo3:Nuqata}) and possessive~(\ref{Glo3:Manamka}) pronouns. With all three, case marking attaches to the same stem; nominative case is zero-marked.\\
A: word: ñuqa-ta	 gloss: I-ACC
B: word: ishkay	 gloss: two
C: word: ñuqa-pa-kta	 gloss: I-GEN-ACC
D: word: ñuqa-ta-s	 gloss: I-ACC-ADD
Please only return the letter (A–D). Do not say anything else.
Correct Answer: A

Here is a relevant knowledge point for this example, with the related morphemes and glosses masked: In this case, it is generally suffixed with one of the evidentials \phono{-mi} or \phono{-shi} and indicates that the sentence it heads is closely related to the sentence that precedes it.\footnote{As an anonymous reviewer points out, forms such as \phono{chay-mi} and \phono{chay-shi} are lexicalized discourse markers, and, as such “they do not take productive affixes such as \phono{-kuna}, \phono{-pi}, or \phono{-man}” among others.} \SYQ{} demonstrative pronouns are identical in form to the demonstrative determiners~(\ref{Glo3:Kaymillwa}--\ref{Glo3:Waktrakrayqa}).\\
A: word: chay-kuna	 gloss: DEM.D-PL
B: word: ka-ra	 gloss: be-PST
C: word: wak-qa	 gloss: DEM.D-TOP
D: word: wak	 gloss: DEM.D
Please only return the letter (A–D). Do not say anything else.
Correct Answer: D

Here is a relevant knowledge point for this example, with the related morphemes and glosses masked: These pronouns are dependent in the sense that they cannot occur uninflected: the suffixes of the nominal (possessive) paradigm attach to dependent pronouns indicating the person and --~in the case of the first person~-- sometimes the number of the referent of the pronoun (\phono{llapa-nchik} ‘all of us’). Dependent pronouns function in the manner as personal pronouns do: they may refer to any of the participants in an event, subject~(\ref{Glo3:Sikya}) or object~(\ref{Glo3:Chaykuska}); they inflect obligatorily for case~(\ref{Glo3:Hukrunata}) and optionally for number; and they may be followed by enclitics~(\ref{Glo3:Kikinkamatr}).\\
A: word: pampa-ku-y-ni-n-pa	 gloss: bury-REFL-INF-EUPH-3-LOC
B: word: tilivisyun-ta	 gloss: television-ACC
C: word: (llapa-yki)	 gloss: all-2

Please only return the letter (A–D). Do not say anything else.
Correct Answer: D

Question 56:
You are a linguist specializing in Yauyos Quechua. You are given a sentence along with its morpheme breakdown, gloss, and translation. Words are separated by spaces, and morphemes are separated by hyphens. However, a word and its gloss are missing and represented by an underscore. Based on your understanding, please choose the most appropriate option. 
Sentence (with missing item): ___ willa-ma-nchik
Gloss (with missing item): ___ tell-1.OBJ-1PL
The English translation of this sentence is:Who’s going tell us?
Here is a relevant knowledge point for this example, with the related morphemes and glosses masked: \SYQ{} has seven interrogative-indefinite stems: \phono{pi} ‘who’, \phono{ima} ‘what’, \phono{imay} ‘when’, \phono{may} ‘where’, \phono{imayna} ‘how’, \phono{mayqin} ‘which’, \phono{imapaq} ‘why’, and \phono{ayka} ‘how much or how many’, as shown in Table~\ref{Tab9}. These form interrogative~(\ref{Glo3:Pitaq}--\ref{Glo3:Chaypaqa}), indefinite~(\ref{Glo3:Pipis}--\ref{Glo3:Ayvisdiman}), and negative indefinite pronouns~(\ref{Glo3:Manapipis}--\ref{Glo3:Rayaqa}). Interrogative pronouns\index[sub]{pronouns!interrogative} are formed by suffixing the stem --~generally but not obligatorily~-- with any of the enclitics \phono{-taq}, \phono{-raq}, \phono{-mI}, \phono{-shI} or \phono{-trI} (\phono{the morpheme ___} ‘who’, \phono{ima-raq} ‘what’); indefinite pronouns\index[sub]{pronouns!indefinite} are formed by attaching \phono{-pis} to the stem (\phono{pi-pis} ‘someone’, \phono{ima-pis} ‘something’); negative indefinite pronouns\index[sub]{pronouns!negative indefinite}, by preceding the indefinite pronoun with \phono{mana} ‘no’ (\phono{mana pi-pis} ‘no one’, \phono{mana ima-pis} ‘nothing’).
A: word: may-taq	 gloss: where-SEQ
B: word: pi-taq	 gloss: who-SEQ
C: word: pantalun-ni-yuq	 gloss: pants-EUPH-POSS
D: word: chay-pi-taq	 gloss: DEM.D-LOC-SEQ
Please only return the letter (A–D). Do not say anything else.
Correct Answer: B

Here is a relevant knowledge point for this example, with the related morphemes and glosses masked: Any numeral, \lsc{num}, may be suffixed with any plural possessive suffix\index[sub]{numerals!with possessive suffixes} --~\phono{-nchik}, \phono{-Yki}, or \phono{-n}. These constructions translate ‘we/you/they \lsc{num}’ or ‘the \lsc{num} of us/you/them’ (\phono{kimsanchik} ‘we three’, ‘the three of us’)~(\ref{Glo3:Ishkaynin}).\\
A: word: mutu-wan-shi	 gloss: motorcycle-INSTR-EVR
B: word: ishkay-ni-nchik	 gloss: two-EUPH-1PL
C: word: ishkay-ni-n	 gloss: two-EUPH-3
D: word: huk-ni-n	 gloss: one-EUPH-3
Please only return the letter (A–D). Do not say anything else.
Correct Answer: C

The English translation of this sentence is:I left my little vicuña with my father.
Here is a relevant knowledge point for this example, with the related morphemes and glosses masked: In the first person singular, the noun \phono{papa} ‘father’ inflects \phono{papa-ni-y} to refer to one’s biological or social father~, (\ref{Glo3:Vikunachayta}).\footnote{An anonymous reviewer writes, “As a loan word, most Central Quechuan languages have papa: with final vowel length (reinterpretation of final accent in Spanish ‘\spanish{papá}’). As such, \phono{-ni} is required before a syllable-closing suffix, such as \phono{-y}. Though \phono{papa} does not end in a long vowel in SYQ, it probably did at one time, and the effect is retained.”}\\
A: word: papa-ni-y-wan	 gloss: father-EUPH-1-INSTR
B: word: kusas-ni-nchik-kuna-lla-ta-tr	 gloss: things-EUPH-1PL-PL-RSTR-ACC-EVC
C: word: yaku-kta	 gloss: water-ACC
D: word: papa-ni-n	 gloss: father-EUPH-3
Please only return the letter (A–D). Do not say anything else.
Correct Answer: A

Question 206:
You are a linguist specializing in Yauyos Quechua. You are given a sentence along with its morpheme breakdown, gloss, and translation. Words are separated by spaces, and morphemes are separated by hyphens. However, a word and its gloss are missing and represented by an underscore. Based on your understanding, please choose the most appropriate option. 
Sentence (with missing item): Dimunyu-m chay-qa chay altu ___ uku-n-paq yatra-n
Gloss (with missing item): Devil-EVD DEM.D-TOP DEM.D high ___ inside-3-LOC live-3
The English translation of this sentence is:It was a devil. It~\dots{} lives in the stone up inside it.
Here is a relevant knowledge point for this example, with the related morphemes and glosses masked: In all dialects, \phono{-paq} is often used in place of \phono{-pa} and \phono{-pi} as both a locative~(\ref{Glo3:Dimunyum}) and genitive~(\ref{Glo3:Imapaypaq}); in the \CH{} dialect, \phono{-traw} is used in addition to \phono{-pa} and \phono{-pi} as a locative~(\ref{Glo3:Pustatrawshi}),~(\ref{Glo3:Nuqakunaqa}). As a genitive, \phono{-pa} can usually be translated ‘of’ or with a possessive pronoun; as a locative, it can usually translated ‘in’ or ‘on’.\\
A: word: matray-pi	 gloss: cave-LOC
B: word: rumi-pis	 gloss: stone-ADD
C: word: rumi-paq	 gloss: stone-LOC
D: word: pampa-ku-y-ni-n-pa	 gloss: bury-REFL-INF-EUPH-3-LOC
Please only return the letter (A–D). Do not say anything else.
Correct Answer: C

Words are separated by spaces, and morphemes are separated by hyphens. However, a word and its gloss are missing and represented by an underscore. Based on your understanding, please choose the most appropriate option. 
Sentence (with missing item): qayna ___ mas-mi
Gloss (with missing item): previous ___ more-EVD
The English translation of this sentence is:It’s more than yesterday.
Here is a relevant knowledge point for this example, with the related morphemes and glosses masked: In comparative expressions, \phono{-paq} attaches to the base of comparison~(\ref{Glo3:Qayna}),~(\ref{Glo3:Celia}); it may be combined with the Spanish-origin comparatives \phono{mihur} (\phono{mejor} ‘better’) and \phono{piyur} (\phono{peor} ‘worse’)~(\ref{Glo3:Pular}). It can generally be translated ‘for’; in its capacity as a purposive, it can generally be translated ‘in order to’.\\
A: word: puntraw-pi	 gloss: day-LOC
B: word: ingaña-ma-ra	 gloss: trick-1.OBJ-PST
C: word: puntraw-paq	 gloss: day-ABL
D: word: tuma-y-ta	 gloss: take-INF-ACC
Please only return the letter (A–D). Do not say anything else.
Correct Answer: C

Question 244:
You are a linguist specializing in Yauyos Quechua. You are given a sentence along with its morpheme breakdown, gloss, and translation. Words are separated by spaces, and morphemes are separated by hyphens. However, a word and its gloss are missing and represented by an underscore. Based on your understanding, please choose the most appropriate option. 
Sentence (with missing item): ___ qutu-naku-la-nchik
Gloss (with missing item): ___ gather-RECIP-PST-1PL
The English translation of this sentence is:We women gathered amongst ourselves.
Here is a relevant knowledge point for this example, with the related morphemes and glosses masked: \phono{-puRa}\index[sub]{exclusive} --~realized \phono{-pula} in the \CH{} dialect~(\ref{Glo3:Walmipula}) and \phono{-pura} in all others~-- indicates the inclusion of the marked individual among other individuals of the same kind. It can be translated as ‘among’ or ‘between’. \phono{-puRa} is not commonly employed; more commonly employed is the particle \phono{intri} ‘between’, borrowed from Spanish (\spanish{entre} ‘between’)~(\ref{Glo3:warmiqa}).\\
A: word: ishkay-ni-nchik	 gloss: two-EUPH-1PL
B: word: walmi-pula	 gloss: woman-EXCL
C: word: walmi-pis	 gloss: woman-ADD
D: word: warmi-m	 gloss: woman-EVD
Please only return the letter (A–D). Do not say anything else.
Correct Answer: B

Here is a relevant knowledge point for this example, with the related morphemes and glosses masked: It can generally be translated ‘because’, ‘because of’ or ‘on account of’. \phono{-rayku} is not frequently employed: ablative \phono{-paq} is more frequently employed to indicate motivation or reason~(\ref{Glo3:Qatra}), although this \phono{-paq} does not, as an anonymous reviewer points out, mark the same relation. \phono{-kawsu} (\Sp~\spanish{causa} ‘cause’) may be employed in place of \phono{-rayku}~(\ref{Glo3:Manamlichi}). Recognized but not attested spontaneously outside \AMV{} and \CH.\\
A: word: ka-sa-n-pi	 gloss: be-PRF-3-LOC
B: word: qayku-sa-n-paq	 gloss: corral-PRF-3-ABL
C: word: qipi-ku-sa-n-ta	 gloss: carry-REFL-PRF-3-ACC
D: word: urqu-pi	 gloss: hill-LOC
Please only return the letter (A–D). Do not say anything else.
Correct Answer: B

Question 253:
You are a linguist specializing in Yauyos Quechua. You are given a sentence along with its morpheme breakdown, gloss, and translation. Gloss (with missing item): ___ look-REFL-PROG-3 ball play-AG-PL-ACC-EVD
The English translation of this sentence is:They’re watching television, ball players.
Here is a relevant knowledge point for this example, with the related morphemes and glosses masked: In the \CH{} dialect, the accusative\index[sub]{accusative} is realized \phono{-kta} after a short vowel and \phono{-ta} after a long vowel or consonant~(\ref{Glo3:piluta}),~(\ref{Glo3:apakunki}); in all other dialects it is realized as \phono{-ta} in all environments. \phono{-ta} indicates the object or goal of a transitive verb~(\ref{Glo3:Asnuqa}),~(\ref{Glo3:maqarura}).\\

B: word: imayna	 gloss: how
C: word: tilivisyun-ta	 gloss: television-ACC

Please only return the letter (A–D). Do not say anything else.
Correct Answer: C

Gloss (with missing item): ___ come-SUBIS Qaquna-3-ACC come-SUBIS-ADD
The English translation of this sentence is:Coming via the interior. Coming via Qaquna.
Here is a relevant knowledge point for this example, with the related morphemes and glosses masked: With \phono{-na} nominalizations, \phono{-ta} may be omitted. In many instances, \phono{-ta} does not indicate accusative case. \phono{-ta} may indicate the goal of movement of a person, as in~(\ref{Glo3:Siqashpaqa}) and~(\ref{Glo3:rirqani}), \phono{-n-ta} may indicate \lsc{path}~(\ref{Glo3:shamushpa}) (see also~§\sectref{sssec:simhina}, ex.(~\ref{Glo3:kunachatam})).\footnote{Thanks to Willem Adelaar for pointing this out to me.}\\
A: word: ka-sa-:-paq	 gloss: be-PRF-1-ABL
B: word: uku-ta	 gloss: inside-ACC
C: word: uku-n-ta	 gloss: inside-3-ACC
D: word: utrku-n-ta	 gloss: hole-3-ACC
Please only return the letter (A–D). Do not say anything else.
Correct Answer: C

Here is a relevant knowledge point for this example, with the related morphemes and glosses masked: \phono{-wan} may mark any animate individual who takes part in an event together with the performer~(\ref{Glo3:Taytachalla}),~(\ref{Glo3:Imapaqmi}); it may also mark the actor in an event referred to by a causative verb~(\ref{Glo3:Manaraqmi}).\\

The English translation of this sentence is:Help me tomorrow or tomorrow mine and then we’ll plant yours.
Here is a relevant knowledge point for this example, with the related morphemes and glosses masked: Combinations\index[sub]{case!combinations} of case suffixes are rare. They do occur, however, notably with \phono{-pa}, \phono{-wan}, and \phono{-hina}. Where a noun phrase marked with genitive \phono{-pa} or \phono{-paq} functions as an anaphor, the phrase may be case marked as its referent would be~(\ref{Glo3:Paqarin}),~(\ref{Glo3:Piluntaqa}). Note that in ~(\ref{Glo3:Piluntaqa}) the accusative has no phonological reflex in the English gloss.\\

Question 307:
You are a linguist specializing in Yauyos Quechua. You are given a sentence along with its morpheme breakdown, gloss, and translation. Words are separated by spaces, and morphemes are separated by hyphens. However, a word and its gloss are missing and represented by an underscore. Based on your understanding, please choose the most appropriate option. 
Sentence (with missing item): wak urqu ___ karu karu-ta-m muyu-mu-nchik
Gloss (with missing item): DEM.D hill ___ far far-ACC-EVD circle-CISL-1PL
The English translation of this sentence is:We circle around very far behind that hill.
Here is a relevant knowledge point for this example, with the related morphemes and glosses masked: Noun-noun relations more specific than the ‘in’ and ‘of’, for example, of \phono{-pi} and \phono{-pa} are expressed by noun phrases headed by nouns which name relative positions (see \sectref{ssec:locnouns} on locative nouns)~(\ref{Glo3:karutam}--\ref{Glo3:pasayan}). Such nouns include, for example, \phono{qipa} ‘rear’; \phono{hawa} ‘top’; and \phono{trawpi} ‘center’. The head (relational) noun is inflected for person, agreeing with the noun to which it is related; this noun may be inflected with genitive \phono{-pa} (\phono{pantyun\pb{-pa} qipa-\pb{n}} ‘behind the cemetery’ lit. ‘of the cemetery its behind’).\\
A: word: qipa-n-pa	 gloss: behind-3-LOC
B: word: siki-paq	 gloss: behind-LOC
C: word: urqu-pi	 gloss: hill-LOC
D: word: qipa-n-ta-ña-taq	 gloss: behind-3-ACC-DISC-SEQ
Please only return the letter (A–D). Do not say anything else.
Correct Answer: A

The English translation of this sentence is:My aprons and things with them are dry.
Here is a relevant knowledge point for this example, with the related morphemes and glosses masked: \phono{-sHa} is perfective, deriving stative participles. It is realized as \phono{-sa} in \ACH, \AMV{}, and \SP{} and as \phono{-sha} in \LT{} and \CH. \phono{-sHa} nominalizations form adjectives (\phono{the morpheme ___} ‘dried’)~(\ref{Glo3:Mandilllaykunaqa}--\ref{Glo3:Wakrunapa}) as well as relative (\phono{apa-sa-y} ‘that I bring’)~(\ref{Glo3:dividisan}--\ref{Glo3:Ratuskamanshi}), and complement clauses (\phono{atipa-sha-y-ta} ‘what I can’)~(\ref{Glo3:Imatataq}--\ref{Glo3:Nuqapataqa}).\\
A: word: huk-kuna-paq	 gloss: one-PL-BEN
B: word: wasi-kuna-man-shi	 gloss: house-PL-ALL-EVR
C: word: chaki-sa	 gloss: dry-PRF
D: word: chaki-sha-m	 gloss: dry-PRF-EVD
Please only return the letter (A–D). Do not say anything else.
Correct Answer: C

Here is a relevant knowledge point for this example, with the related morphemes and glosses masked: \phono{-kuna\tss{2}} indicates that the referent of its base is accompanied by another entity, generally of the same class (\phono{qusa-yki-\pb{kuna}} ‘your husband and all’)~(\ref{Glo3:Ispusu}--\ref{Glo3:Pachamanka}).\\

D: word: mama-:-kuna	 gloss: mother-1-PL
